5 , A ut ho r r et ai ns  fu ll ri gh ts . Key fingerprint = AF19 FA27 2F94 998D FDB5 DE3D F8B5 06E4 A169 4E46                              Key fingerprint = AF19 FA27 2F94 998D FDB5 DE3D F8B5 06E4 A169 4E46
Kayron C. Valentine Version 1.2e Encrypting File System Primer: Basics and Best Practices Introduction With the introduction of Windows 2000, Microsoft implemented a number of security-based improvements aimed at making their flagship operating system (OS) more robust and attractive for enterprise deployment.  They’ve built on existing techno ogy from NT 4.0 and added several features, which take advantage of standards that for years have been proven effective in the industry.  The result is an OS less dependent of flawed proprietary standards, such as NTLM and WINS, which have been plagued with numerous vulnerabilities and over the years have made the job of system security an even more difficult task.
Among the many improvements in Windows 2000, one of the more notable ones is the addition of the Encrypting File System (EFS).  EFS is a means of protecting user data which takes advantage of two well known industry standards: Data Encryption Standard X (DESX) and RSA public key exchange.  It is supported in all versions of Windows 2000, and works transparently (without user input) to encrypt and decrypt files for user access.  In the past, a user would have to encrypt and decrypt files using a third party application which had to be purchased and installed separately on their system, and which most often depended on the user entering a correct password.  With EFS, this is no longer a necessity.  As always however, most security measures are only effective if your users are properly informed, and EFS is no exception.  As such, we’ll take a look at EFS, what it is, how it works, and more importantly, what you need to know to make it work  effectively for you. EFS Basics EFS is a Windows 2000 file encryption technology which is based on the public-key encryption standard.   It works in conjunction with NTFS permissions to grant and deny users access to files and folders.  EFS can be particularly beneficial to users most vulnerable to data theft through the physical removal of their system or its hard drive.  An example of this would be laptop users.  It is also attractive because there are no added cost, overhead, or possible compatibility complications that are usually associated with third party encryption software, and the encryption and decryption procedures are greatly simplified yet very secure.
In it’s current implementation EFS uses Data Encryption Standard X (DESX) to encrypt the actual data.  However, future releases of the OS are expected to allow alternate encryption schemes to be substituted for stronger security.  For it’s initial release, DESX was selected because it is a symmetrical encryption scheme, meaning that the same key is used to encrypt and decrypt the data.  This in itself is weak but is faster than most other encryption techniques, which makes it perfect for encrypting larger data files with reduced ©  S A N S In st itu te  2 00 0
2 00
5 , A ut ho r r et ai ns  fu ll ri gh ts . Key fingerprint = AF19 FA27 2F94 998D FDB5 DE3D F8B5 06E4 A169 4E46                              Key fingerprint = AF19 FA27 2F94 998D FDB5 DE3D F8B5 06E4 A169 4E46
overhead.
Encryption and decryption can be initiated by two different means.  The first method is to encrypt and decrypt files using the Windows Explorer GUI by right mouse clicking on a file or folder and performing a file -> properties then selecting the advanced button, and selecting the appropriate checkbox.  The second method is by running the cipher.exe utility at the command line.  To understand the encryption process, take a look at the table below, which gives a detailed account of what takes place when either of these tasks is performed. EFS Encryption Process Summary Step in Sequence Process The user profile loads to the Registry, if necessary1. EFS creates a log file named efsX.log in the System Volume Information 2. subdirectory.  X is a unique number in the filename (e.g.,, efs0.log).  EFS writes to the log file when performing subsequent steps in the encryption process so that EFS can recover the file in case of system failure during the encryption process. Microsoft Base Cryptographic Provider generates a random 128-bit File 3. Encryption Key (FEK) for the file (128 bit in the US and Canada only). EFS reads the KEY_CURRENT_USER\Software\Microsoft\WindowsNT\4. CurrentVersion\EFS\CurrentKeys\CertificateHash Registry value to identify the user’s public key/private key pair. EFS creates a Data Decryption Field (DDF) key ring with an entry for the 5. user and associates the key ring with the file.  The entry contains a copy of the FEK that the user’s EFS public key encrypted. EFS creates a Data Recovery Field (DRF) key ring for the file with an 6. entry for each Recovery Agent on the system.  Each entry contains a copy of the FEK that the Recovery Agent’s EFS public key encrypted. EFS creates a backup file, efsX.tmp, in the directory in which the file 7. undergoing encryption resides.  X is a unique number in the filename (e.g., efs0.tmp). EFS places the DDF and DRF key rings in a header and adds the header to 8. the file as the file’s EFS attribute. EFS marks the backup file as encrypted and copies the original file to the 9. backup file. EFS destroys the original file’s contents and copies the backup to the 10. original file.  The copy operation results in the data’s encryption, because the backup file is marked as encrypted. EFS deletes the backup file.11. EFS deletes the log file.12. ©  S A N S In st itu te  2 00 0
2 00
5 , A ut ho r r et ai ns  fu ll ri gh ts . Key fingerprint = AF19 FA27 2F94 998D FDB5 DE3D F8B5 06E4 A169 4E46                              Key fingerprint = AF19 FA27 2F94 998D FDB5 DE3D F8B5 06E4 A169 4E46
The user profile unloads from the Registry if it loaded in step 1.13. A copy of the table above is located at the following URL: http://www.win2000mag.com/Articles/Print.cfm?ArticleID=5592. Strengths and Weaknesses EFS will automatically encrypt files copied or moved to directories that have been marked as encrypted.  This is beneficial because encrypting a folder allows users to save files to a specific location on their hard drives and know that they will be encrypted, without having to perform the process manually each time.  This is one of the many features that could be considered a pro or strength of E S.  In fact, E S has many strengths, and also some inherent weakness as well, a few of which are listed here. Strengths EFS is embedded in the Windows 2000 kernel.ü EFS protects a user’s encryption key from ever being written to a system pagefile.  ü Because the EFS drivers are integrated into the kernel they are protected from ü direct user access. Currently, there are no known methods of circumventing EFS by direct access to ü the hard drive using NTFSDOS, an NTFS driver for Linux, or similar methods. EFS works seamlessly in the background, encrypting and decrypting files without ü user input, thus a user needs only know your login password to decrypt your files.  Users will never forget to encrypt a file that they’ve decrypted for access, leaving it unprotected and vulnerable. EFS (more specifically, the OS) is flexible and allows for upgrades to stronger ü encryption processes and the use of smart cards for private key storage in the future. Weaknesses EFS only works on Windows 2000 systems with the new NTFS 5.0 partition.  ü Currently, there is no support for NTFS in Windows NT 4.0, FAT16, FAT32, ext2fs, or other types of partitions found on other operating systems either by Microsoft or other vendors. EFS does not support the encryption of system files.  Encrypted system files may ü cause your system to become unusable. Third party backup utilities are not guaranteed to work on EFS encrypted files.ü EFS relies heavily on the premise that a strong authentication practice is already in ü place in order be most effective. Antivirus software must run with master keys which enable it to decrypt files ü while scanning and updating virus definition files EFS works seamlessly in the background, encrypting and decrypting files without ü ©  S A N S In st itu te  2 00 0
2 00
5 , A ut ho r r et ai ns  fu ll ri gh ts . Key fingerprint = AF19 FA27 2F94 998D FDB5 DE3D F8B5 06E4 A169 4E46                              Key fingerprint = AF19 FA27 2F94 998D FDB5 DE3D F8B5 06E4 A169 4E46
user input, thus a user needs only know your login password to decrypt your files.  Access to your login means access to your encrypted files with no further effort. ©  S A N S In st itu te  2 00 0

5 , A ut ho r r et ai ns  fu ll ri gh ts . Key fingerprint = AF19 FA27 2F94 998D FDB5 DE3D F8B5 06E4 A169 4E46                              Key fingerprint = AF19 FA27 2F94 998D FDB5 DE3D F8B5 06E4 A169 4E46
EFS Best Practices and Recommendations These recommendations are intended for both the user and the systems administrator and could go a long way toward maximizing the effectiveness of EFS on your systems.  A list of Microsoft’s recommended best practices can be found on their website at http://support.microsoft.com/support/kb/articles/Q223/3/16.asp; these have been incorporated into this list as well. Laptop and standalone users are not encouraged to try changing the default 1. recovery agent.  Changing the default recovery agent is somewhat simplified when your system is a domain member and when Active Directory (AD) is setup and running.  However, because EFS needs to generate a private/public key pair it requires a Certificate Authority, and as such, at least one certificate server to change the default recovery agent.
Download the efsinfo utility from Sysinternals and familiarize yourself with its 2. usage.  This utility has the ability to tell you who encrypted a file as well as which users have the ability to decrypt it as well.  This is available for download at http://www.sysinternals.com/ntw2k/source/misc.shtml.  Keep in mind that by default all a user needs are read/write NT permissions to encrypt a file.  It is then possible for a user to inadvertently or maliciously encrypt key documents or other files on your organization’s system.  This utility will help you determine who the culprit is, and can also help you recover a file should a user’s FEK be lost or inaccessible. Encrypt the “My Documents” folder for all users, which is the default storage 3. location for most user-generated documents. Encrypt the “Temp” folder to protect your user’s documents when they are 4. temporarily stored during editing. Encrypt folders instead of the individual files.  Create a folder and designate it as 5. encrypted.  This prevents inadvertent decryption especially during editing and with the “Save As” feature in most software.  “Save As” will only save the new document as encrypted if it is saved to a folder that has been marked as encrypted.  Otherwise, using “Save As” while working on an encrypted document removes encryption from the new file that is created. Generate your private keys on a physically secure system.6. Outline a backup procedure for your private keys, especially for your local 7. administrator account on your standalone systems and laptops.  One way of accomplishing this is by exporting these to a PFX file and storing them on a floppy disk in a secure location.  Instructions on exporting your private keys can ©  S A N S In st itu te  2 00 0
2 00
5 , A ut ho r r et ai ns  fu ll ri gh ts . Key fingerprint = AF19 FA27 2F94 998D FDB5 DE3D F8B5 06E4 A169 4E46                              Key fingerprint = AF19 FA27 2F94 998D FDB5 DE3D F8B5 06E4 A169 4E46
be found on the Microsoft website at http://support.microsoft.com/support/kb/articles/Q241/2/01.asp. Designate two or more recovery agents per Organizational Unit (OU).8. Be careful not to designate too many users as recovery agents.  EFS will generate 9. as many DRFs as there are recovery agents.  This information is then stored with the file (generating larger files) and could cause a significant loss in system performance.  Furthermore, each addition recovery agent is one more user who may not be following proper security precautions and whose password may be compromised, opening your files and other users’ for easy access by an intruder.  Remember that designated recovery agents do not need to know your private key to gain access to your encrypted data.  This also limits the number of users that you can know for sure may gain access to your files.  Remember that not all domain administrators are designated recovery agents, so even though NTFS permissions give them permission to view your file or maybe even take ownership and change permissions, they do not have access to a key which can decrypt your data and are still unable to gain full access. Disable the usage of print spool files.  These files are not encrypted and can easily 10. be compromised by a would-be intruder. Develop a recovery agent archive program to ensure that encrypted files can be 11. recovered using obsolete recovery keys.  Again, recovery certificates and private keys must be stored in a secure location (not your desk draw or on a floppy disk in your system). Always adhere to strong password practices.  EFS is by no means a substitute for 12. password authentication or NTFS permissions.  Encrypted files are safe as long as your private keys are not compromised.  In some instances it would be far easier to compromise a user’s weak password, or that of the default administrator account, and gain access to your encrypted files simply by logging on with these credentials. For standalone systems, which are not members of a domain, change the default 13. Syskey setting to use the system password option instead of storing the key locally in the system registry.  In Windows 2000 the OS uses Syskey to encrypt the password hash, which (speaking of the hash) can easily be compromised using utilities such as L0phtcrack.  However, the possibility still exists for an intruder to remove the encrypted hash and replace it with an un-encrypted one, to which he knows the password.  By default, Windows will boot and encrypt this un-encrypted hash using Syskey, allowing the intruder to access your system using a password, which he has chosen.  Storing the password on a floppy disk is especially not recommended for laptop users who may me tempted to leave this ©  S A N S In st itu te  2 00 0
2 00
5 , A ut ho r r et ai ns  fu ll ri gh ts . Key fingerprint = AF19 FA27 2F94 998D FDB5 DE3D F8B5 06E4 A169 4E46                              Key fingerprint = AF19 FA27 2F94 998D FDB5 DE3D F8B5 06E4 A169 4E46
floppy disk in the drive thus defeating the purpose. Download and update your copy of cipher.exe from the Microsoft website.  14. Whenever a file is encrypted or moved to an encrypted folder, a plain text copy of the file is made for the purposes of recovery should an error occur during this process.  Although these plain text copies are deleted, fragments of the file are still accessible until the location where that files was stored is overwritten either by another file or by wiping this disk space.  Wiping the disk would be good practice, however, most third party disk-wiping utilities fail to wipe disks formatted with Windows 2000’s NTFS file system.  The new version of cipher.exe now includes the ability to wipe deleted data from your hard disk.  This is available for download at http://support.microsoft.com/support/kb/articles/Q298/0/09.asp.  Be sure to follow the instructions for installation. Do not compress your encrypted files and/or folders.  Compression and EFS are 15. what you might call mutually exclusive, that is, it’s either one or the other.  This is apparent if you are encrypting or compressing files using the Window Explorer GUI as selecting one option automatically deselects the other.  However, this is not obvious to users who may choose to use the command line utilities.  Therefore, it is possible that users may inadvertently remove encryption from files.  If compression is applied to a folder marked as encrypted, the system prompts the user and asks if they would like to compress and decrypt the folder, and whether or not to apply this to all files and subfolders as well.  If however, the user is working within an encrypted folder, this warning will not appear if compression is applied to a single file or an empty (newly created) folder.  The parent folder is not affected, however, these files and folders are no longer protected when being accessed and moved even though they reside within a folder marked as encrypted.  Encrypting a compressed file will remove compression. Conclusion EFS is new technology built on proven technology, built with the expectation of considerable improvements as the standards and technology evolves.  It’s present shortcomings may be attributed to it’s implementation which causes unexpected side effects, vulnerabilities, and compatibility woes with third party software packages such as virus scanners, backup utilities, and disk wiping software.  But EFS is one more step toward preventing intruders from gaining access to your important data, one more hurdle that an intruder must overcome to compromise the integrity of your system, and coupled with other security measures, is one more example of the advantages of defense in depth. ©  S A N S In st itu te  2 00 0
2 00

Source: Extents and Extent allocation in Ext4 | linux - Oracle Blogs
URL: https://blogs.oracle.com/linux/extents-and-extent-allocation-in-ext4
Extents and Extent allocation in Ext4 | linux Skip to content Accessibility Policy Oracle Oracle Linux Blog Search Exit Search Field Clear Search Field Menu CATEGORIES Oracle Back Oracle Open Cloud Infrastructure Oracle Linux RELATED CONTENT Hardware Cert. List ISV Catalog Developers GitHub Blogs Home RSS Oracle Linux Blog Follow: RSS Facebook Twitter LinkedIn YouTube Instagram Facebook Twitter LinkedIn Email Linux Kernel Development, Technologies Extents and Extent allocation in Ext4 October 15, 2024 9 minute read Srivathsa Dara Introduction In a filesystem, a file might seem like it is stored continuously on disk, but that's not the case. The contents of a file are logically continuous but may not be physically contiguous and can be scattered across different locations on the disk. This poses a challenge for the filesystem when managing the creation, growth, and deletion of files. In this blog, we'll explore how ext4 addresses this challenge using extents to map logical blocks to physical blocks. We'll also look at the different steps involved in extent allocation in an ext4 filesystem, and finally, we'll discuss the significance of delayed allocation. By the end of this blog, you'll understand how filesystems manage space while keeping files logically continuous, even when their data is scattered across the disk. Let's get started with an example. Example: Let's assume, on a fresh filesystem three files (say, file1, file2, and file3) are created one after another, each occupying 16 KiB. On disk, they might look like this: As of now, the contents of each file are stored continuously on-disk. Now, let' say the user adds more data to file2. To accommodate this new data, a new on-disk block is needed. However, as seen in the image, file2 is surrounded by file1 on one side and file3 on the other. So, where can the new data be placed? One option is to move file3 by one on-disk block, thus creating space for file2 to use. This may seem like a good idea, but it is very inefficient. In a filesystem with hundreds or thousands of files, this approach would involve moving numerous files just to make space for a single physical block. The approach that filesystems use is to allocate the next available physical block on the disk and update the extent map (if the filesystem uses extents) or the block addressing map (if the filesystem uses direct/indirect addressing). The filesystem logically maps this newly allocated physical block as the 5th logical block of the file2. The disk looks something like this after allocation:
The contents of the file2 is not physically continuous but they are continuous logically. The animation below shows how files are allocated on-disk space over time. As the files grow, available blocks are allocated.
Extent-Based Mapping vs. Direct/Indirect Block Addressing: In indirect/direct block addressing, logical and physical blocks are mapped one-to-one, whereas in extent-based mapping, a range of logical blocks are mapped to a range of physical blocks using a single extent structure. Here's how the extents for file1, file2, and file3 look like: Copy code snippet Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy File 1: Extent Logical Blocks Physical Blocks Length 0 0 - 3 100 - 103 4 File 2: Extent Logical Blocks Physical Blocks Length 0 0 - 3 104 - 107 4 1 4 112 1 File 3: Extent Logical Blocks Physical Blocks Length 0 0 - 3 108 - 111 4 Extent structure in an ext4 filesystem: Copy code snippet Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy struct ext4_extent { __le32 ee_block; /* First logical block that the extent covers / __le16 ee_len; / Number of blocks covered by this extent / __le16 ee_start_hi; / High 16 bits of the starting physical block / __le32 ee_start_lo; / Low 32 bits of the starting physical block */ }; For example, if an extent has the following values: Copy code snippet Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy struct ext4_extent { __le32 ee_block = 5; __le16 ee_len = 10; __le16 ee_start_hi = 0; __le32 ee_start_lo = 100; }; That means the extent maps logical blocks 5 to 15 of the file to physical blocks 100 to 110 on disk. For more information about extents, refer to the “Extent Tree” section of Understanding Ext4 Disk Layout, Part2 blog, where a detailed explanation of extents is provided. Now we know what an extent is and its purpose. Let's dive into the extent allocation algorithm in ext4. We'll cover how an extent is allocated, where the filesystem starts searching when a request is made, and what happens if there isn't enough space to fulfill the request. Extent Allocation Ext4 follows a simple algorithm to allocate extents. When a request to allocate physical blocks for an inode is made, the process of extent allocation starts. The first step in this process is finding the hint. What is a hint? A hint is a physical block number calculated by the filesystem. The search for free space starts from the hint block. The method for finding the value of the hint block is different for a new inode and an existing inode. The calculation is done as follows: Hint For Newly Created Inodes: The hint for a newly created inode comes from the block group in which the inode is located. The exact block number of the hint block is calculated as follows: Copy code snippet Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy hint = (first block of the inode's block group) + [(current->pid)%16 * (number of blocks present in the inodes block group)/16] Example: Let's assume in a 1GiB filesystem, an inode present in the fourth block group is initialized for use, and let's assume the PID of the current process is 1863. The number of blocks in the fourth block group of 1GiB filesystem is 32,768. The first block number of the fourth block group is 131,072. Therefore, the hint can be calculated as follows: Copy code snippet Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy hint = 131,072 + [(1863)%16 * (32768)/16] = 131,072 + [7 * 2048] = 145,408 So, the hint for this inode will be the physical block 145,408. As mentioned earlier, this block comes from the same block group in which the inode is present, which is the fourth block group in this case. Hint For Existing Inodes: The hint for an existing inode will be the physical block adjacent to the last extent of the inode. Example: Consider the extent map of an inode: Copy code snippet Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy Level Entries Logical Physical Length Flags 0/ 0 1/ 2 0 - 2047 2916352 - 2918399 2048 0/ 0 2/ 2 2048 - 2559 2946560 - 2947071 512 Here, the last extent of the inode spans from the physical block 2946560 to 2947071. The adjacent physical block to the last allocated extent is 2947072. Therefore, the hint for this inode will be 2947072. Block Search and Allocation The filesystem uses the hint to determine where to start looking for free blocks. The ext4 filesystem maintains block bitmaps and buddy bitmaps for each block group. The block bitmap tracks which blocks are in use and which are free. The buddy bitmap tracks the buddy information, such as the number of buddies available of different orders. You can see the buddy information of the filesystem in the file /proc/fs/ext4/<device>/mb_groups . The snippet below is part of the mb_groups from an ext4 filesystem: The mb_groups file displays the buddy information for each block group within the filesystem. Each column represents number of free buddies available of sizes 4kiB, 8KiB, 16KiB (Order of 2). In the highlighted first block group, we observe that it contains 4 4KiB buddies (2^0 * 4KiB (blocksize) ), 5 8KiB buddies (2^1 * 4KiB), and so on. The filesystem first loads the block bitmap and buddy bitmap of the hint block's block group and checks if the hint block is in use or not. If the Hint Block is Free: Case 1: There is enough contiguous free space starting from the hint block to satisfy the requested size. In this case these blocks will be allocated and are marked as used in both the buddy bitmap and the block bitmap. The allocation ends here. Case 2: There isn't enough contiguous space, meaning hint block is available but there isn't enough continuous space starting from the hint block to satisfy the requested size. In this case, if the flag EXT4_MB_HINT_MERGE is set, the physical blocks available so far are used. If the flag EXT4_MB_HINT_MERGE is not set, these blocks are ignored, and the search continues If the Hint Block is Not Free or in Case 2 of the above scenario where EXT4_MB_HINT_MERGE is not set: In these cases the filesystem will search through every block group to find free space. The search starts from the block group of the hint block and progresses through each block group in the filesystem. Example: Let's assume the filesystem to be 1GiB in size(It has 8 block groups in total). If the block group of the hint block is 4. In this case, the search progresses in this order: 4->5->6->7->0->1->2→3. For each block group, the buddy bitmaps are loaded, and a check is done to find the best possible contiguous buddies. The best possible contiguous buddies are those that are contiguous and whose sum of sizes is closest to the requested size. If an exact match is found, it's used. If not, the buddies that are closest to the requested size will be chosen. The sum of the sizes of these buddies may be lower or higher. If their sum is more than the requested size, the buddies will be split into lower-order buddies to match the requested size. If their sum is lower than the requested size, then the buddies found so far will be used and added to the extent tree. For the remaining blocks, the process for extent allocation goes back to step 1 (Calculation of hint), and this process continues until the requested size is found. If there isn't enough space, then ENOSPACE will be returned. The maximum extent size supported by ext4 is 128 MiB, which is the size of a block group. If a request is made for 150 MiB, even if there is 150 MiB of contiguous space available, the allocation will be split into two steps. First, 128 MiB will be allocated (as this is the maximum extent size in ext4), and for the remaining 22 MiB, the algorithm will loop back and try to allocate it. Delayed allocation Ext4 supports delayed allocation, it is also the default allocation algorithm. In delayed allocation, on-disk blocks are not allocated immediately after a write operation. Instead, the buffer heads are marked with the BH_Delay flag and the physical blocks are allocated later during the write-back process. The reason is that delaying the allocation could result in the accumulation of more data, which causes the allocation of more contiguous physical blocks. As a result, the number of seek operations is reduced, fragmentation is minimized, and the overall performance of the filesystem is improved. It also has an advantage with short-lived files (e.g., temp files), the files might be deleted before allocation even happens. To disable delayed allocation, the filesystem has to be mounted with nodelalloc option. Filesystem without Delayed Allocation: Copy code snippet Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy [bash]# mount -o nodelalloc /dev/sdc1 /media/ [bash]# [bash]# cd /media [bash]# date; dd if=/dev/random of=test bs=4k count=10 Tue Aug 27 13:22:25 GMT 2024 <================= 10+0 records in 10+0 records out 40960 bytes (41 kB, 40 KiB) copied, 0.000281584 s, 145 MB/s [bash]# date; debugfs -R "ex test" /dev/sdc1 Tue Aug 27 13:22:27 GMT 2024 <================= debugfs 1.45.6 (20-Mar-2020) Level Entries Logical Physical Length Flags 0/ 0 1/ 1 0 - 9 34304 - 34313 10 [bash]# To mount a filesystem without delayed allocation use (-o nodelalloc). A file named test was created, and the debugfs ex command was used to see it's allocated extents. We see that physical blocks were allocated immediately (see the time, there was only a 2-second gap). Filesystem with Delayed Allocation: Copy code snippet Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy Copied to Clipboard Error: Could not Copy [bash]# mount /dev/sdc1 /media [bash]# [bash]# cd /media [bash]# [bash]# date; dd if=/dev/random of=test2 bs=4k count=10 Tue Aug 27 13:25:23 GMT 2024 <================= 10+0 records in 10+0 records out 40960 bytes (41 kB, 40 KiB) copied, 0.00021046 s, 195 MB/s [bash]# [bash]# [bash]# date; debugfs -R "ex test2" /dev/sdc1 Tue Aug 27 13:25:26 GMT 2024 <================= debugfs 1.45.6 (20-Mar-2020) Level Entries Logical Physical Length Flags [bash]# [bash]# [bash]# date; debugfs -R "ex test2" /dev/sdc1 Tue Aug 27 13:26:32 GMT 2024 <================= debugfs 1.45.6 (20-Mar-2020) Level Entries Logical Physical Length Flags 0/ 0 1/ 1 0 - 9 33795 - 33804 10 [bash]# A file named test2 was created on a filesystem with delayed allocation enabled. When the debugfs ex command was run immediately afterward (just a 3-second gap), and it did not output any extents. That is because, with delayed allocation, the physical blocks are not allocated immediately. However, after some time (~ 1min), we can see from the debugfs ex command that the physical blocks were allocated. Summary In this blog, we explored how filesystems use logical-to-physical mapping to present data continuously to the user. We covered the different steps of the extent allocation process, which involve finding hints and searching for free space. At the end, we also discussed the importance of delayed allocation in improving performance and reducing fragmentation. References https://ext4.wiki.kernel.org/index.php/DelayedAllocation https://blogs.oracle.com/linux/post/understanding-ext4-disk-layout-part-2 Linux Source Code Authors Srivathsa Dara Previous post Automate Virtual Machine Migrations Using virt-v2v with Oracle Linux Automation Manager Monica S | 2 minute read Next post Oracle Linux 6 Extended Support Ends Soon—Don't Wait, Plan Your Upgrade Today Gursewak Sokhi | 2 minute read Resources for About Careers Developers Investors Partners Startups Why Oracle Analyst Reports Best CRM Cloud Economics Corporate Responsibility Security Practices Learn What is Customer Service? Contact us 0 Login Register Homepage blog Operating Systems Operating Systems File Systems Comparison: NTFS, ext4, APFS and ZFS September 9, 2025 22 min read Andrés Delgado This blog post compares the different file systems NTFS, ext4, APFS, and ZFS, examining in detail the key features, advantages, and uses of each. It covers the fundamental concepts of file systems, the advantages of NTFS, the performance of ext4, the innovative features of APFS, and the high-capacity architecture of ZFS. The key differences between file systems are highlighted, and the importance of file system selection for data security is emphasized. The article also includes expert opinions, guiding readers to determine the most suitable file system for their needs. What are File Systems? Basic Concepts Content Map Toggle What are File Systems? Basic Concepts What is NTFS? Its Key Features and Advantages ext4 File System: Performance and Reliability APFS' Innovative Features and Use Cases ZFS: High-Capacity and Secure File System Key Differences Between File Systems Different File System Features Comparison According to Usage Areas Selecting File Systems Based on Usage Areas The Importance of File Systems for Data Security Expert Opinions on File Systems Conclusion: Which File System is Right for You? Frequently Asked Questions File systemsFile systems are the fundamental structures that determine how data is organized, stored, and accessed on a storage device. They define how files and directories (folders) are managed on storage media such as a computer's hard drive, SSD, or USB memory stick. These systems provide a framework for the operating system to read and write data meaningfully. An effective file system must optimize performance while maintaining data integrity. File systems not only store files but also store information about file properties (creation date, size, permissions, etc.), called metadata. This metadata helps manage and locate files efficiently. Different file systems can support different metadata attributes, resulting in significant differences in compatibility and functionality. Basic Properties of File Systems Data organization and storage File and directory management Access permissions and security Data integrity and recovery mechanisms Performance optimization Metadata management Different operating systems support different file systems. For example, Windows typically uses NTFS (New Technology File System), while Linux uses file systems such as ext4 (Fourth Extended File System) or XFS. macOS uses APFS (Apple File System). Each file system has its own advantages and disadvantages, and it's important to choose one based on your usage scenario. What are File Systems? Basic Concepts The choice of file system directly impacts performance, security, and compatibility. For example, a file system designed to store large files may perform differently than one optimized for storing small files. Similarly, in an environment requiring security measures, a file system with encryption and access control capabilities should be preferred. In the following sections, we will examine each of these file systems more closely, detailing their key differences and applications. What is NTFS? Its Key Features and Advantages File Systems NTFS (New Technology File System), a key feature among these, was developed by Microsoft and became widespread with the Windows NT family of operating systems. It is considered the core file system of modern Windows systems. NTFS offers significantly more advanced features than previous FAT (File Allocation Table) file systems and is well-suited to today's high-capacity storage solutions. NTFS, an advanced file system, offers a combination of security, reliability, and performance. It's significantly more effective than FAT systems, particularly when it comes to managing large files and disks. It also stands out with features like file permissions, encryption, and disk quotas. What is NTFS? Its Key Features and Advantages The features offered by NTFS make it a preferred file system, particularly in corporate environments and personal computers. File compression saves disk space, while volume shadowing simplifies regular backups. All these features make NTFS a solution that meets the expectations of a modern file system. Key Advantages of NTFS Advanced security features (file permissions, encryption) High reliability and data recovery capabilities Support for large file and disk sizes Simplify disk space management with disk quota Saves storage space with file compression feature Easy backup with volume shadowing NTFS is a comprehensive file system that combines the security, reliability and performance features required by modern operating systems. Data security In any environment where storage efficiency is important, NTFS stands out as a reliable solution. ext4 File System: Performance and Reliability ext4, or Fourth Extended Filesystem, is a file system widely used in Linux systems. Developed as the successor to ext3, ext4 has the capacity to support larger file systems and larger files. File Systems It stands out among the market with its performance and reliability. Its advanced features make it designed to meet modern storage needs. ext4 offers an effective solution, especially for large volumes and high-performance applications. It provides faster read/write speeds with improved disk space management and less fragmentation. Additionally, checksumming Thanks to its feature, it protects data integrity and prevents possible data loss. ext4 File System: Performance and Reliability | | | --- | --- | --- | | Feature | Explanation | Benefits | | Large File Support | Supports individual file sizes up to 16TB. | Ideal for large media files and databases. | | Advanced Disk Space Management | It uses extent-based space allocation. | Provides less fragmentation and better performance. | | Checksumming | Performs integrity checks for data and metadata. | It prevents data loss and increases reliability. | | Delayed Allocation | Delays the writing of data blocks to disk. | Provides better write performance and less fragmentation. | Advantages and Disadvantages of ext4 Advantages: High performance and speed Extended file system sizes Enhanced data integrity (Checksumming) Better disk space management Widespread support and compatibility Disadvantages: May not have as advanced features as NTFS or APFS Data recovery can be difficult in some cases ext4, It is still the default file system of many Linux distributions today. It's used extensively, from server systems to desktop computers. It's a preferred option, especially in environments requiring stability and performance. The open-source nature of the file system allows for continuous development and improvement, ensuring ext4 remains relevant for many years to come. The Ext4 file system is an ideal choice for users seeking reliability, performance, and broad compatibility. It provides a strong foundation for data storage and management, especially on Linux-based systems. Maintaining data integrity and offering high performance togetheris one of the main features that make ext4 stand out. Innovative Features and Uses of APFS Developed by Apple, APFS (Apple File System) is a modern file system designed specifically for use in macOS, iOS, iPadOS and watchOS operating systems. File Systems APFS, a standout among the latest offerings, offers significant performance and security improvements over the previous HFS+ system. While optimized for SSD (Solid State Drive) storage, APFS can also run effectively on traditional HDD (Hard Disk Drive) storage. This file system aims to make data management more efficient and secure, particularly on devices within the Apple ecosystem. Innovative Features and Uses of APFS APFS is designed to be compatible with modern storage technologies and specifically leverages the performance advantages of SSDs. With its cloning feature, copies of files and directories can be created almost instantly, saving storage space and speeding up backups. Snapshots, on the other hand, record the state of the file system at a specific point in time, allowing for easy recovery in the event of data loss. These features: data integrity and increases safety. Things to Consider When Using APFS Keep Encryption Key Safe: If APFS encryption is used, it's crucial to keep the encryption key in a safe place. Losing the key can make data inaccessible. Consider SSD Optimization: APFS is optimized for SSDs. If using HDDs, avoid regular disk defragmentation to improve performance. Check Snapshots Regularly: Regularly checking snapshots and deleting those that are not needed ensures efficient use of storage space. Follow Updates: Regular updates to APFS are important for performance and security. Create a Backup Strategy: Despite the features offered by APFS, regular backups are the most reliable method against data loss. In terms of security, APFS maximizes data security with its strong encryption features. Files and directories can be protected with modern encryption algorithms such as AES (Advanced Encryption Standard), providing a significant defense against unauthorized access. Furthermore, thanks to its space-sharing feature, multiple volumes can be created on a single physical partition, and each volume can be used for different purposes. This is a significant advantage, especially in multi-user systems or when separate storage areas are required for different projects. APFS is a modern file system designed for devices in the Apple ecosystem, focused on performance, security, and flexibility. Innovative features like cloning, snapshots, encryption, and space sharing simplify data management and increase security. APFS These features make it an attractive option, especially for Apple users. ZFS: High-Capacity and Secure File System ZFS is a file system and a database server developed by Sun Microsystems and later made open source. Data management It's a powerful solution that combines features. It stands out with its advanced data integrity, high capacity, and flexible management features. It's an ideal choice for enterprise-level data storage and backup solutions. Unlike traditional file systems, ZFS uses advanced checksums to ensure data consistency and can automatically detect and fix data corruption. One of the key features of ZFS is the concept of pools. Pools allow physical disks to be combined to create a single, large storage area. This not only increases storage capacity but also offers the opportunity to optimize data redundancy and performance. For example, it integrates with RAID configurations, providing a high level of protection against disk failures. Furthermore, ZFS simplifies data backup and recovery with advanced features like snapshots and cloning. Critical Advantages of ZFS Data Integrity: Prevents data corruption with advanced checksums. High Capacity: It can support petabytes of data. RAID Integration: Provides data redundancy against disk failures. Snapshots: It simplifies data backup and recovery processes. Cloning: It speeds up data copying and creating test environments. ZFS, especially businesses and stands out for its advantages for large data centers. It's an ideal option for organizations that prioritize data security and consistency and require high-capacity storage solutions. However, installation and configuration processes can be more complex and demanding of system resources (especially RAM) than other file systems. Therefore, it's important to ensure that system requirements are met before using ZFS. ZFS: High-Capacity and Secure File System | | | --- | --- | --- | --- | | Feature | ZFS | ext4 | NTFS | | Data Integrity | Advanced checksums | Checksum support | Journaling | | Capacity | 16 Exabytes | 1 Exabyte | 256 TB (in practice) | | RAID Support | Onboard RAID (RAID-Z) | Software RAID (mdadm) | Software RAID | | Snapshot | Resident | With LVM | VSS (Volume Shadow Copy Service) | ZFS is a powerful storage medium with high capacity, data integrity and advanced management features. file system It's an excellent option, especially for enterprise-level data storage and backup solutions. However, complex installation and high system requirements should be taken into account. Key Differences Between File Systems File systemsFile systems are the underlying software layers that manage how data is stored, organized, and accessed. Each file system has its own advantages, disadvantages, and design philosophy. These differences affect various factors such as performance, reliability, scalability, and compatibility. For example, NTFS (New Technology File System) is commonly used in Windows operating systems, while ext4 (Fourth Extended File System) is widely preferred in Linux systems. APFS (Apple File System) is used in macOS and iOS devices. ZFS (Zettabyte File System) is designed for high-capacity storage solutions and situations requiring data integrity. The key differences between file systems lie in their data structures, metadata management, and the features they support. For example: Journaling: Logging file system operations prevents data loss in case of system crashes. Snapshots: Saving the state of the file system at a specific point in time is important for data recovery. Data Integrity Checks: It uses checksums and error-correcting codes to prevent data from becoming corrupted. The following table compares the key features of different file systems: Key Differences Between File Systems | | | --- | --- | --- | --- | | File System | Developer | Main Areas of Use | Highlights | | NTFS | Microsoft | Windows operating systems, external disks | Security features, compression, encryption | | ext4 | Linux community | Linux operating systems, servers | Performance, reliability, expandability | | APFS | Apple | macOS, iOS, iPadOS | Fast, modern, snapshots, cloning | | ZFS | Sun Microsystems (Oracle) | Servers, NAS devices, high-capacity storage | Data integrity, RAID support, unified storage management | These differences make each file system more suitable for certain usage scenarios. For example, if security is a priority, NTFS may be a better option, while ext4 may be preferred in server environments that require high performance and reliability. APFS offers seamless integration and modern features within the Apple ecosystem, while ZFS is ideal for large data centers and complex storage needs. Different File System Features The technical specifications between file systems create significant differences in performance and ease of use. For example, Journaling This feature maintains the consistency of the file system by preventing data loss during sudden system shutdowns. Snapshots It allows system administrators and users to easily restore data. Data compression and encryption Features such as these ensure efficient use of storage space and protection of sensitive data. Comparison According to Usage Areas The choice of file system depends on the application and needs. A simple and user-friendly file system may be sufficient for home users, while businesses and servers may require a more complex and reliable file system. For example: Choosing the right file system in data storage solutions is a critical decision that directly affects system performance and data security. A file system that offers high read and write speeds is preferred for gaming PCs, while a file system that can efficiently manage large files may be more suitable for media servers. Therefore, when choosing a file system, consider performance, reliability, security and scalability Factors such as should be taken into consideration. Selecting File Systems Based on Usage Areas TRUE file system Choosing a file system is a critical decision in terms of performance, reliability, and compatibility. Each file system is designed to meet different needs and usage scenarios. For example, NTFS is commonly used in Windows operating systems, while ext4 is preferred in Linux systems. APFS is optimized for modern Apple operating systems. ZFS is ideal for high-capacity storage solutions and situations requiring data integrity. Steps to Consider When Choosing a File System Operating System Compatibility: The selected file system must be fully compatible with the operating system to be used. Performance Requirements: The read/write speeds required by applications and data should be taken into consideration. Reliability and Data Integrity: To prevent data loss, fault tolerance and data recovery capabilities should be evaluated. Storage Capacity and Scalability: It is important that the file system is capable of meeting future storage needs. Security Features: Encryption and access control mechanisms should be examined to protect data against unauthorized access. One file system Its performance depends on the type and intensity of applications it will run. For example, for bandwidth-intensive tasks like video editing, more advanced file systems such as ZFS or APFS may be preferred. Conversely, for lighter office applications and general use, NTFS or ext4 may be sufficient. In terms of reliability, ZFS and APFS offer more advanced features for maintaining data integrity and recovering from errors. Selecting File Systems Based on Usage Areas | | | --- | --- | --- | | File System | Recommended Areas of Use | Advantages | | NTFS | Windows operating systems, general purpose use, gaming | Wide compatibility, security features, compression | | ext4 | Linux servers, Android devices, development environments | High performance, reliability, expandability | | APFS | macOS and iOS devices, SSD storage, video editing | Fast performance, snapshots, encryption | | ZFS | Data storage servers, virtualization, backup systems | Data integrity, RAID support, high capacity | Data security is also file system is an important factor in choosing a file system. Features such as encryption, access control, and data recovery play a critical role in protecting sensitive data. Especially in corporate environments, choosing file systems with advanced security features like ZFS can minimize the risk of data loss. Therefore, file system When choosing, factors such as operating system compatibility, performance, reliability and security should be carefully evaluated. The Importance of File Systems for Data Security Data security is more critical than ever in today's digital age. For businesses and individuals, data protection is crucial not only for meeting legal requirements but also for reputation management and operational continuity. This is where: file systems This is where choosing the right file system can help prevent potential data loss and security breaches by ensuring data is stored and accessed securely. The impact of a file system on data security is multifaceted. First, file systems manage access permissions to data, ensuring only authorized users can access it. Advanced file systems protect data with encryption features and prevent data loss with mechanisms that ensure data integrity. For example, some file systems facilitate data backup and recovery processes, helping to keep data safe even in unexpected situations. The Importance of File Systems for Data Security | | | --- | --- | --- | | File System Feature | Contribution to Data Security | Sample Application | | Access Control Lists (ACL) | It prevents unauthorized access and ensures that only authorized users can access data. | Restrict access to sensitive data with detailed permission settings in NTFS. | | Encryption | It prevents unauthorized persons from accessing information by making the data unreadable. | Encrypt the entire disk or specific files with the built-in encryption feature in APFS. | | Data Integrity Checks (Checksum) | It prevents data loss by detecting data corruption or modification. | Continuous checksum checks are performed to ensure data integrity in ZFS. | | Snapshots | It provides the opportunity to quickly restore data in case of data loss by taking a copy of the data at a specific point in time. | Easily revert to previous versions in case of data loss by taking snapshots in ZFS. | file systems It's a cornerstone of data security. A reliable file system helps keep your business or personal data safe by protecting it from unauthorized access and ensuring data integrity. Therefore, it's crucial to consider your data security needs when choosing a file system. Remember, choosing the right file system is one of the best investments you can make for your long-term data security. Here are some important factors to consider when choosing the right file system: Encryption Capabilities: Whether the file system offers native encryption support. Access Control Mechanisms: Ability to define detailed access permissions on a user and group basis. Data Recovery Features: Ease of snapshots and data backup/restore. Data Integrity: Protection mechanisms against data corruption. Experts emphasize the importance of considering not only technical specifications but also usage scenarios, scalability requirements, and long-term maintenance costs when choosing the right file system. For a system administrator, this means finding a flexible and reliable solution that can meet ever-changing needs. Today, NTFS, ext4, APFS and ZFS Different file systems, such as .NET, offer different advantages. For example, NTFS offers broad compatibility and security features in Windows environments, while ext4 provides performance and stability in Linux systems. APFS offers an optimized solution for modern Apple devices, while ZFS prioritizes data integrity and large storage capacities. Experts note that each file system has its own unique strengths, and the choice should be based on specific needs. Expert Opinions on File Systems | | | --- | --- | --- | | File System | Highlights | Expert Comment | | NTFS | Wide Compatibility, Security | Ideal for Windows environments, but performance may be limited on Linux. | | ext4 | High Performance, Stability | It is an excellent choice for Linux servers and desktop systems. | | APFS | Optimization, Fast Actions | Designed for the Apple ecosystem, it delivers high performance with SSDs. | | ZFS | Data Integrity, High Capacity | It is suitable for situations requiring large storage areas and data security. | Experts also note that file systems are not just a technical specification, but also reflect the data management philosophy of a business or individual. Data security, backup strategies and disaster recovery plans Issues like these are directly related to file system selection. Therefore, it's crucial to consider long-term strategic goals when choosing a file system. It's important to remember that choosing the right file system can prevent data loss, improve performance, and reduce operating costs. Experts, file systems are constantly evolving and they emphasize the emergence of new technologies. Therefore, being knowledgeable about file systems and staying up-to-date is critical to a successful data management strategy. Conclusion: Which File System is Right for You? File systems The choice depends on many factors, from your personal needs to your hardware specifications and even your operating system preferences. Each file system has its own advantages and disadvantages. Therefore, it's difficult to say which file system is best; the key is to determine what works best for you. For example, if you're using Windows and compatibility with external drives is your priority, NTFS might be a good option. However, if you're looking for high performance and flexibility in a Linux-based system, ext4 might be a better fit. Conclusion: Which File System is Right for You? | | | --- | --- | --- | | File System | Recommended Areas of Use | Highlights | | NTFS | Windows system drives, external disks (for Windows compatibility) | Wide compatibility, security features, file compression | | ext4 | Linux system drivers, servers | High performance, expandability, reliability | | APFS | macOS system drives, SSDs | Fast performance, data copy efficiency, security | | ZFS | Large storage systems, servers, data centers | High capacity, data integrity, RAID-like features | Things to Consider When Making a Choice Operating System Compatibility: What operating system are you using? Make sure the file system is fully compatible with that operating system. Performance Requirements: Need fast read/write speeds? If you're using an SSD, an optimized file system like APFS might be better. Security Requirements: Do you need advanced security features to protect your data? File systems like ZFS or NTFS may offer better options. Storage Capacity: Are you working with very large files? High-capacity file systems like ZFS might be more suitable. Data Integrity: Is preventing data loss critical to you? ZFS is designed to ensure data integrity. Data security is more important than ever today. Therefore, when choosing a file system, you should consider not only performance but also the ability to maintain data integrity. For example, ZFS, thanks to its advanced data integrity features, is particularly suitable for environments storing critical data. Meanwhile, APFS, optimized for modern SSDs, offers macOS users a fast and reliable experience. TRUE file system The file system you choose can directly impact your system's performance, security, and overall stability. Therefore, it's important to carefully consider the factors mentioned above and choose the file system that best suits your needs. Remember, every system is different, and the best solution is the one that meets your specific needs. Frequently Asked Questions Why are file systems important and how do they affect our daily use? File systems determine how data is stored, organized, and accessed. Choosing the right file system directly impacts system performance, data security, and storage capacity. In everyday use, file system performance can impact application opening speeds, file transfers, and overall system responsiveness. What are the most obvious advantages of NTFS and in what scenarios should it be preferred? NTFS, developed by Microsoft, is the default file system for Windows operating systems. Its most prominent advantages are its security features (file and folder permissions), support for large file and disk sizes, and robustness. It is often preferred in Windows-based systems, especially in environments requiring security and compatibility. What are the key performance and reliability features that distinguish the ext4 file system from others? ext4 is a widely used file system on Linux systems. For performance, it offers improved block allocation methods and faster file system checks. For reliability, it reduces the risk of data loss thanks to its advanced journaling feature. What are the innovative features that make the APFS file system different from others, and on which devices is it particularly suitable for use? APFS (Apple File System) is Apple's modern file system. Its key innovative features include snapshots, cloning, and encryption. It's optimized for SSDs and flash storage, making it particularly suitable for Mac computers, iPhones, and other Apple devices. What are the strengths of the ZFS file system in terms of data integrity and high capacity management, and what types of servers is it ideal for? ZFS is a file system that offers high-capacity storage, data integrity, and advanced RAID-like features. It can automatically detect and repair data corruption, making it ideal for mission-critical servers such as data storage servers, virtualization platforms, and backup systems. What are the key differences between different file systems, and what consequences do these differences have in terms of performance, security, and compatibility? Key differences include supported file and disk sizes, security features (permissions, encryption), performance optimizations (block allocation, caching), and compatibility (operating systems and hardware). These differences directly impact the file system's overall performance, data security, and compatibility with different platforms. Which file systems make the most sense for which usage scenarios? For example, which file system would be best suited for a gaming PC, an office PC, or a NAS device? For gaming PCs, NTFS (Windows) or ext4 (Linux) may be more suitable options; for office PCs, NTFS (Windows) or APFS (macOS); and for NAS devices, ZFS (data integrity and RAID features) or ext4 (performance and widespread use). The choice depends on the operating system, performance requirements, and data security expectations. What is the role of file systems in terms of data security and which file systems are more effective in preventing data loss? File systems play a crucial role in data security by maintaining data integrity, facilitating backups, and encrypting data. ZFS (data integrity checking and repair) and APFS (encryption and snapshots) may be more effective at preventing data loss. However, regular backups are always the most important security measure.
